1	"""Naive PyTorch reference for Kimi Delta Attention (KDA) forward, chunk form.
2	
3	This is the correctness oracle, NOT the SOTA baseline. It mirrors the
4	chunk-parallel formulation in fla/ops/kda/naive.py (Songlin Yang et al.)
5	without any Triton or CUDA optimization.
6	
7	Inputs (per the FLA convention):
8	  q, k : (B, T, H, K)   bf16   -- queries / keys
9	  v    : (B, T, H, V)   bf16   -- values
10	  g    : (B, T, H, K)   fp32   -- per-channel log-decay (in-chunk cumsum applied)
11	  beta : (B, T, H)      bf16   -- write strength
12

14	  o    : (B, T, H, V)   bf16
15

2	display_name: "Kimi Delta Attention (chunk forward) via CUTLASS CuTe"
3	precision: bf16
4	regime: compute
5	
6	# Approximate FLOPs per forward call. KDA forward (chunk form) breakdown per
7	# token across all heads:
8	#   - intra-chunk Aqk (q @ k.T with decay): 2*BT*K
9	#   - intra-chunk Akk + cumulative invert : ~3*BT*K + BT^2
10	#   - w = A @ (g.exp()*k):                  2*BT*K
11	#   - u = A @ v:                            2*BT*V
12	#   - inter-chunk q @ S:                    2*K*V
13	#   - inter-chunk (Aqk @ v_i):              2*BT*V
14	#   - state update S += k.T @ v_i:          2*K*V
15	# Aggregating with a constant of 4 (typical for chunked linear attention) gives
16	# the formula below. Used only for roofline reporting -- not graded directly.
17	flops_formula: "4 * B * T * H * (K*V + CHUNK_SIZE*K + CHUNK_SIZE*V)"
18	
19	# Bytes moved (lower bound): one read of q,k,v,g,beta and one write of o.
20	bytes_formula: "B*T*H*K*2 + B*T*H*K*2 + B*T*H*V*2 + B*T*H*K*4 + B*T*H*2 + B*T*H*V*2"

Now I understand the problem. I need to implement Kimi Delta Attention (KDA) forward in chunk form as a custom CUDA/Triton kernel. Let me analyze the reference implementation carefully: The reference implementation does: 1. Rearranges q, k, v, g, beta into chunks of size `chunk_size` (64) 2. Applies cumulative sum on g along the chunk dimension (intra-chunk cumsum) 3. Computes intra-chunk A matrix (K-K interaction, lower triangular with diagonal masked) 4. Does a recurrent pass over chunks (inter-chunk recurrence) 5. The state S is (B, H, K, V) and gets updated chunk by chunk Let me work through the math more carefully. **Intra-chunk computation:** For each chunk: - `A[..., i, :] = einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", k * (g - g_i).exp(), k_i)` — this is computing `k * exp(g - gi) @ ki.T` for each row i in the chunk - `A = A * beta[..., None]` — scale by beta - `A = -A.masked_fill(mask_diag_upper, 0)` — negate and zero upper triangular and diagonal - Then a cumulative product to get the full intra-chunk transition matrix - `A = (A + I) * beta[..., None, :]` — add identity and scale by beta - `w = A @ (g.exp() * k)` and `u = A @ v` **Inter-chunk recurrence:** State S has shape (B, H, K, V). For each chunk i from 0 to NT-1: - Compute Aqk: `Aqk[..., j] = einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", q_i * (g_i - g_j).exp(), k_j)` for each j in the chunk — this is query-key interaction with decay, upper triangular (strictly, since position j has seen only earlier positions plus self) - Actually wait, looking more carefully: Aqk is masked with `mask_strict_upper` (upper triangular, diagonal=1), so it only keeps the strictly upper triangular part - `v_i = u_i - w_i @ S` - `o[:, :, i] = (q_i * g_i.exp()) @ S + Aqk @ v_i` - State update: `S = S * g_i_last.exp() + (g_i_last - g_i).exp() * k_i.T @ v_i` Wait, let me re-read more carefully. The state update is: ``` S = S * rearrange(g_i[:, :, -1].exp(), "b h k -> b h k 1") S = S + rearrange((g_i[:, :, -1:] - g_i).exp() * k_i, "b h c k -> b h k c") @ v_i ``` So: S_new = S_old * exp(g_last) + ((exp(g_last - g_i)) * k_i)^T @ v_i where g_last is the last element in the chunk (cumsum applied, so it represents the decay up to the end of the chunk). Let me make sure I understand g. g is a per-channel log-decay with in-chunk cumsum already applied. Then `g = g.cumsum(-2)` is applied inside the function. So g accumulates within each chunk. Actually wait — the docstring says "per-channel log-decay (in-chunk cumsum applied)". But then the reference code does `g = g.cumsum(-2)`. That seems like it's doing an additional cumsum. Let me re-read... The input says "g of shape (B, T, H, K) in fp32 (per-channel log-decay with in-chunk cumsum already applied)". But then the code does `g = g.cumsum(-2)` AFTER rearrange. Hmm, maybe the comment is slightly off, or the cumsum in the input is the raw decay and the code does the actual cumsum. Actually, looking at it more carefully: g is a per-channel log-decay. The input g from `get_inputs()` is just random small negative numbers. The "in-chunk cumsum already applied" might refer to how g is typically used in FLA (where the cumsum is applied before calling chunk_kda), but in the reference, `g.cumsum(-2)` is applied within the function to create a monotonically decreasing (more negative) decay along the chunk dimension. So the flow is: 1. Input g: raw per-channel log-decay values 2. After rearrange + cumsum: cumulative decay within each chunk, so exp(g) gives monotonically decreasing values (decay from 1 down to smaller values) Then: - `exp(g_i - g_j)` where i > j means `exp(g_i) / exp(g_j)` — since g is cumsum and monotonically decreasing, g_i > g_j when i > j (well, actually g_i is more negative when i > j since decay accumulates)... Wait, g values are negative (log-decay). So cumsum makes later positions more negative. exp(more negative) = smaller. So g_i (later position) < g_j (earlier position). Then g_i - g_j < 0, and exp(g_i - g_j) < 1. That makes sense — it's an exponential decay. OK so the decay factor for position j relative to position i (where i > j, meaning query i attends to key j) is exp(g_i - g_j) = exp(g_i) / exp(g_j). Since g_i < g_j (more decay accumulated at later positions), this ratio < 1. Actually wait, I need to think about the cumsum direction. After `g = g.cumsum(-2)`, g accumulates along the chunk's position dimension. Since the raw g values are negative, cumsum makes each position more negative than the last. Now for the intra-chunk computation: - `A[..., i, :] = einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", k * (g - g_i).exp(), k_i)` - This is: for each target position i, for each source position j: `k_j * exp(g_j - g_i) * k_i` - If j > i: g_j < g_i (more decay later), so g_j - g_i < 0, exp < 1 - If j < i: g_j > g_i, so g_j - g_i > 0, exp > 1 — WAIT, this seems wrong. If j < i (past positions), decay should be < 1, not > 1. Hmm, let me re-check. After cumsum, g is monotonically decreasing (more negative). Position 0 has the least negative value, position BT-1 has the most negative. For attention from query i to key j: - If j < i: g_j > g_i (since g_j is less negative). Then g_j - g_i > 0. exp(g_j - g_i) > 1. That seems wrong — the decay should make past positions LESS important. Wait, maybe the decay convention is different. In Kimi Linear/KDA, the delta rule uses exp(-|g_i - g_j|) or something? Or maybe the sign convention is that g is positive and represents accumulated decay? Actually, let me not overthink the signs. The reference code is what it is, and I need to reproduce its output exactly. Let me focus on implementing it efficiently. OK so the key computational patterns are: **Intra-chunk (per chunk):** 1. Compute A matrix: for each i, j in [0, BT): A[i,j] = beta_i * k_j^T * diag(exp(g_j - g_i)) * k_i, masked to lower triangular, negated 2. Actually, A is computed as: A[i] = sum over j of k * exp(g - g_i) * k_i — this gives (B, H, BT, BT) 3. Then cumprod-like operation to get the full transition 4. w = A @ (exp(g) * k), u = A @ v **Inter-chunk (sequential over chunks):** 1. For each chunk, compute Aqk (BT x BT intra-chunk attention of q onto k with decay), masked strictly upper triangular 2. v_i = u_i - w_i @ S (corrected values using state) 3. o_i = (q_i * exp(g_i)) @ S + Aqk @ v_i 4. Update S: S_new = S * exp(g_last) + ((exp(g_last) - g) * k)^T @ v_i Now for the implementation strategy. This is a complex kernel. Let me think about what approach to use. The main operations are batched matrix multiplications: - Intra-chunk: (B*H*NT) batches of (BT, K) @ (K, BT) type operations → O(BT^2 * K) - Intra-chunk: (B*H*NT) batches of A @ (exp(g) * k) → (BT, BT) @ (BT, K) → O(BT^2 * K) - Intra-chunk: (B*H*NT) batches of A @ v → (BT, BT) @ (BT, V) → O(BT^2 * V) - Inter-chunk: for each of NT chunks: (B*H) batches of q @ S → (BT, K) @ (K, V) → O(BT * K * V) - Inter-chunk: for each of NT chunks: Aqk @ v_i → (BT, BT) @ (BT, V) → O(BT^2 * V) - State update: k^T @ v_i → (K, BT) @ (BT, V) → O(K * V * BT) The chunk size is 64, which is small enough that some of these can be done efficiently. Given that this is for SM120 (Blackwell), I think a Triton kernel would be the most practical approach. Let me write a Triton implementation. Actually wait — the problem says "The intended path is CUTLASS CuTe on SM120 but Triton, CUDA C++ via load_inline, or inline PTX are also fine." Triton is explicitly allowed. Let me use Triton since it's much more practical for this complex computation. Let me plan the kernel structure: Since the chunk size is small (64), the key challenge is the inter-chunk recurrence which must be sequential (state S depends on the previous chunk's state). But the intra-chunk computation can be parallelized across all chunks. Here's my plan: 1. Write a Triton kernel for the intra-chunk computation that processes all (B, H, NT) chunks in parallel 2. Write the inter-chunk recurrence that iterates over chunks sequentially, but parallelizes over B and H 3. Use Triton's tl.dot for the matmul operations Let me think about this more carefully. The intra-chunk computation has these steps for each chunk: a) Compute A matrix (BT x BT): For each i in [0, BT): A[i,:] = einsum("c d, d -> c", k * exp(g - g_i), k_i) This is: A_ik = sum_j k_j * exp(g_j - g_i) * k_i, which is (BT, BT) per chunk Then mask upper triangular and diagonal, negate, scale by beta b) Cumulative product: for i in 1..BT-1: A[i, :i] += (A[i, :, None] * A[:, :i]).sum(-2) This is recursive... A[i, j] += sum_{m} A[i, m] * A[m, j] for m < i This is essentially computing (I + tril(A))^(-1) or something similar c) A = (A + I) * beta d) w = A @ (exp(g) * k), u = A @ v The cumulative product step is the trickiest part. Let me analyze it more: ``` for i in range(1, BT): A[..., i, :i] = A[..., i, :i].clone() + (A[..., i, :, None].clone() * A[..., :, :i].clone()).sum(-2) ``` This means: A_new[i, j] = A[i, j] + sum_{m < i} A[i, m] * A[m, j] This is computing something like the matrix exponential or geometric series. If we think of A as a strictly lower triangular matrix (with negative entries), then this recurrence computes (I - A)^{-1} - I, or something like that. Then (A + I) * beta gives the final intra-chunk transition matrix. Actually, let me think about this differently. The recurrence for A after the initial fill: - Initially A has entries only for j < i (lower triangular, excluding diagonal), and these entries are negative (due to the -A masking) - The recurrence computes the "path sum" or geometric series Let's say the initial lower triangular matrix is L (with zeros on diagonal and upper). Then the recurrence computes: A_1 = L A_2 = L + L^2 (since A_2[i,j] = L[i,j] + sum_m L[i,m] * L[m,j]) A_3 = L + L^2 + L^3 ... A_{BT-1} = L + L^2 + ... + L^{BT-1} So after the loop, A = sum_{p=1}^{BT-1} L^p, which equals (I - L)^{-1} - I (since L is strictly lower triangular, L^{BT} = 0). Then: A = (A + I) * beta = (I - L)^{-1} * beta So the intra-chunk transition matrix is beta * (I - L)^{-1}, where L is the negative lower-triangular matrix of k^T * diag(decay) * k weighted by beta. Hmm, this gives me a potential optimization: instead of the iterative cumprod, I could compute (I - L)^{-1} directly. But for BT=64, the iterative approach only takes 64 steps, and each step does a matmul of growing size — but actually, the number of operations for the iterative approach is O(BT^3) per chunk, which is 64^3 = 262K per chunk. For a typical case (B=2, H=8, T=2048, NT=32), that's 2*8*32*262K ≈ 134M FLOPs just for the cumprod, which is actually small. Actually, the cumprod step involves operations that grow in size. Actually, it is: Aqk[i,j] = sum_d q[i,d] * k[j,d] * exp(g[i,d] - g[j,d]) This IS a matmul if we pre-weight: q_weighted[i,d] = q[i,d] * exp(g[i,d]) and k_weighted[j,d] = k[j,d] * exp(-g[j,d]) But wait, the decay is per-channel, so it's: sum_d q[i,d] * k[j,d] * exp(g[i,d] - g[j,d]) = sum_d (q[i,d] * exp(g[i,d])) * (k[j,d] * exp(-g[j,d])) This IS a standard matmul! q_weighted @ k_decayed^T where: - q_weighted[i,d] = q[i,d] * exp(g[i,d]) - k_decayed[j,d] = k[j,d] * exp(-g[j,d]) Wait, but exp(g[i,d] - g[j,d]) = exp(g[i,d]) * exp(-g[j,d]). So yes! Aqk[i,j] = sum_d (q[i,d] * exp(g[i,d])) * (k[j,d] * exp(-g[j,d])) = (q * exp(g)) @ (k * exp(-g))^T That's a huge simplification! The decay in Aqk can be absorbed into the q and k weights, making it a standard matmul. Similarly for the intra-chunk A matrix: A[i,:] = sum_j k_j * exp(g_j - g_i) * k_i = sum_j (k_j * exp(g_j)) * (k_i * exp(-g_i))... wait no. A[..., i, :] = einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", k * (g - g_i).exp(), k_i) This means A[i, j] = sum_d k[j, d] * exp(g[j, d] - g[i, d]) * k[i, d] = sum_d (k[j,d] * exp(g[j,d])) * (k[i,d] * exp(-g[i,d])) = (k * exp(g))[j, :] dot (k * exp(-g))[i, :] So: A = (k * exp(g)) @ (k * exp(-g))^T Wait, let me verify: A[i, j] (output for row i, column j) = k[j]^T diag(exp(g[j] - g[i])) k[i] = sum_d k[j,d] * exp(g[j,d] - g[i,d]) * k[i,d] = sum_d (k[j,d] * exp(g[j,d])) * (k[i,d] * exp(-g[i,d])) So A[i,j] = (k * exp(g))[j] · (k * exp(-g))[i] Which means A = (k * exp(g)) @ (k * exp(-g))^T Where A's row is i (target) and column is j (source). So A[i, j] = how much position i is influenced by position j through key similarity with decay. Wait, let me double-check the indices. k has shape (..., BT, K). k_i has shape (..., K). k * (g - g_i).exp() has shape (..., BT, K). einsum("... c d, ... d -> ... c", k * (g - g_i).exp(), k_i) This contracts over d (K dimension), giving output shape (..., c) = (..., BT). The result is assigned to A[..., i, :]. So A[..., i, j] = sum_d k[..., j, d] * exp(g[..., j, d] - g[..., i, d]) * k[..., i, d] For row i (target position), column j (source position): A[i,j] = k[j]^T @ diag(exp(g[j] - g[i])) @ k[i] Now: k[j]^T @ diag(exp(g[j] - g[i])) @ k[i] = sum_d k[j,d] * exp(g[j,d] - g[i,d]) * k[i,d] = sum_d (k[j,d] * exp(g[j,d])) * (k[i,d] * exp(-g[i,d])) = (k[j] * exp(g[j])) · (k[i] * exp(-g[i])) So A = (k * exp(g)) @ (k * exp(-g))^T Let me verify the shapes: k has shape (B, H, NT, BT, K). k * exp(g) has the same shape. (k * exp(g)) @ (k * exp(-g))^T → (B, H, NT, BT, BT) Which matches A's shape. The convention is A[i,j] = influence of j on i. Then A is masked with mask_diag_upper (=1 for i ≤ j, =0 for i > j), meaning we keep only lower triangular (including diagonal... wait, triu with diagonal=0 means upper triangular including diagonal is True). So masked_fill sets those to 0, keeping strictly lower triangular. Then A = -A. So A_initial = -L where L is strictly lower triangular with entries L[i,j] = (k[j]*exp(g[j])) · (k[i]*exp(-g[i])) * beta_i for j < i. Then the recurrence: ``` A[i, :i] = A[i, :i] + (A[i, :, None] * A[:, :i]).sum(-2) ``` This is: A[i, j] += sum_{m < i} A[i, m] * A[m, j] Finally: A = (A + I) * beta = (sum_{p=0}^{BT-1} L^p where L is the negative lower-triangular matrix) * beta Since L is strictly lower triangular, L^64 = 0, and (I + L + L^2 + ... + L^{BT-1}) = (I - L)^{-1}. So the final A = (I - L)^{-1} * beta, where beta is per-element (diagonal scaling). And w = A @ (exp(g) * k), u = A @ v. OK great, so the intra-chunk A can be computed as: 1. k_exp_g = k * exp(g) [after cumsum] 2. k_exp_neg_g = k * exp(-g) 3. A_raw = k_exp_g @ k_exp_neg_g.transpose(-1, -2) [BT×BT] 4. Apply beta: A_raw = A_raw * beta.unsqueeze(-1) 5. Mask to lower triangular, negate: L = -tril(A_raw, diagonal=-1) 6. A = (I - L)^{-1} * diag(beta) Step 6 is the matrix inverse. For a strictly lower triangular L of size 64×64, (I - L)^{-1} can be computed by forward substitution, which is what the recurrence does. For efficiency, I can: - Compute (I - L)^{-1} * diag(beta) directly using the recurrence - Or compute (I - L)^{-1} first, then multiply by beta - The recurrence (which is effectively forward substitution) takes O(BT^3) operations Actually, for BT=64, computing the inverse via the recurrence is fast enough. This gives True for positions where column > row (strictly above diagonal). Then `.masked_fill(mask_strict_upper, 0)` sets those positions to 0. So positions where j ≤ i (lower triangle + diagonal) survive. This makes sense: when query i attends to key j, we only allow j ≤ i (causal). Now for the intra-chunk A: `mask_diag_upper = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT), diagonal=0)` gives True for positions where j ≥ i (upper triangle + diagonal). `.masked_fill(mask_diag_upper, 0)` sets those to 0. So A keeps only j < i (strictly lower triangular). Then A = -A. This is the "influence" matrix. Position i is influenced by position j ONLY for j < i (the past). Now let me compute the flops for the inter-chunk recurrence more carefully and decide what to optimize. For the inter-chunk, the per-(B,H) per-chunk operations are: 1. Aqk = q_weighted @ k_decayed^T: (BT, K) @ (K, BT) = BT^2 * K * 2 = 64^2 * 128 * 2 = 1,048,576 FLOPs 2. v_i = u - w @ S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) = BT * K * V * 2 = 64 * 128 * 128 * 2 = 2,097,152 FLOPs 3. o_i_term1 = q_exp_g @ S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) = same as above = 2,097,152 FLOPs 4. o_i_term2 = Aqk @ v_i: (BT, BT) @ (BT, V) = BT^2 * V * 2 = 64^2 * 128 * 2 = 1,048,576 FLOPs 5. S_update = k_decayed^T @ v_i: (K, BT) @ (BT, V) = K * BT * V * 2 = 128 * 64 * 128 * 2 = 2,097,152 FLOPs Total per (B,H) per chunk: ~8.4M FLOPs Total for B=2, H=8, T=2048 (NT=32): 8.4M * 16 * 32 = 4.3G FLOPs This is small! The RTX PRO 6000 can do ~90 TFLOPS (bf16), so this takes ~48 microseconds of pure compute. The bottleneck is definitely launch overhead and memory. For maximum performance, I should fuse everything into a single kernel. Let me do that. Let me write a single Triton kernel that: 1. Each block handles one (B, H) pair 2. Loops over all NT chunks 3. For each chunk, does all the operations For the matmuls within the kernel, I'll use tl.dot with tiling. Let me focus on the inter-chunk recurrence kernel. The intra-chunk computation (cumsum, A matrix, w, u computation) can be done in PyTorch since it's fully parallelizable across chunks. OK here's my final plan: ### Final Implementation Plan 1. **Preprocessing (PyTorch):** - Rearrange to chunk form - Apply cumsum to g - Compute intra-chunk A, w, u - Pre-compute q_weighted, k_decayed (for efficiency) 2. **Inter-chunk recurrence (Triton kernel):** - Grid: (B * H,) - Each block loops over NT chunks - For each chunk, compute: - Aqk (decay-weighted q @ k^T), masked to keep lower triangular - v_corrected = u - w @ S - o_chunk = (q * exp(g)) @ S + Aqk @ v_corrected - S = S * decay_factor + k_decayed^T @ v_corrected - Store o_chunk 3. **Postprocessing (PyTorch):** - Rearrange output back to (B, T, H, V) Let me start implementing this. Let me first create a PyTorch implementation to verify correctness, then port the inter-chunk part to Triton. Let me start coding.

(ac-b) = (-b+ac). ✓ Great, so the formula is correct. Now let me implement efficiently. For the intra-chunk computation: 1. k_exp_g = k * exp(g) 2. k_exp_neg_g = k * exp(-g) 3. M_raw = k_exp_g @ k_exp_neg_g^T (BT × BT per chunk), using bmm or einsum 4. M_raw = M_raw * beta.unsqueeze(-1) (row scaling) 5. L = tril(M_raw, diagonal=-1) (positive strictly lower triangular) 6. A = (I + L)^{-1} @ diag(beta) → compute via recurrence or directly invert 7. w = A @ (exp(g) * k) 8. u = A @ v For step 6, I can use the recurrence (like the reference) since BT=64 is small. +"""KDA forward (chunk form) — CUTLASS CuTe / Triton kernel for SM120 Blackwell.
+
+This implements Kimi Delta Attention as a chunk-parallel forward pass using
+Triton for the inter-chunk recurrence. The intra-chunk transition matrix
+is computed in PyTorch with batched operations (efficient cuBLAS matmuls).
+"""
+
+from __future__ import annotations
+
+import torch
+import torch.nn as nn
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
+from einops import rearrange
+
+OP_TYPE = "linear_attention"
+SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
+HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Triton kernel: fused inter-chunk recurrence
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def _kda_inter_chunk_kernel(
+    # Inputs: chunk-form data, all fp32 (pre-converted)
+    q_ptr,          # (B, H, NT, BT, K)  fp32 — q * scale * exp(g)
+    kd_ptr,         # (B, H, NT, BT, K)  fp32 — k * exp(-g) [decayed keys]
+    v_ptr,          # (B, H, NT, BT, V)  fp32
+    g_last_ptr,     # (B, H, NT, K)      fp32 — exp(g[:,:,:,-1,:])
+    w_ptr,          # (B, H, NT, BT, K)  fp32 — from intra-chunk
+    u_ptr,          # (B, H, NT, BT, V)  fp32 — from intra-chunk
+    kg_ptr,         # (B, H, NT, BT, K)  fp32 — k * exp(g) [for S update: (g_last[:,None]-g).exp() * k]
+    # Output
+    o_ptr,          # (B, H, NT, BT, V)  fp32
+    # Dimensions
+    B,
+    H,
+    NT,
+    BT,
+    K,
+    V,
+    # Strides (packed tensors, but we compute explicitly)
+    stride_q_b,
+    stride_q_h,
+    stride_q_n,
+    stride_kd_b,
+    stride_kd_h,
+    stride_kd_n,
+    stride_v_b,
+    stride_v_h,
+    stride_v_n,
+    stride_gl_b,
+    stride_gl_h,
+    stride_gl_n,
+    stride_w_b,
+    stride_w_h,
+    stride_w_n,
+    stride_u_b,
+    stride_u_h,
+    stride_u_n,
+    stride_kg_b,
+    stride_kg_h,
+    stride_kg_n,
+    stride_o_b,
+    stride_o_h,
+    stride_o_n,
+    # Tiling constants
+    BT_TILE: tl.constexpr,
+    VTILE: tl.constexpr,
+    KTILE: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    """
+    Each program (block) handles one (B, H) pair and iterates over all NT chunks.
+
+    State S (K x V) is kept in registers/shared memory.
+    For each chunk n:
+      1. Compute Aqk = tril(q_i @ kd_i^T)  — (BT, BT) intra-chunk attention
+      2. v_corrected = u_i - w_i @ S       — (BT, V)
+      3. o_i = q_i @ S + Aqk @ v_corrected — (BT, V)
+      4. S = S * g_last_i + kg_i^T @ v_corrected — (K, V)
+    """
+    bh_id = tl.program_id(0)
+    b_id = bh_id // H
+    h_id = bh_id % H
+
+    # We process the BT dimension in tiles and V in tiles.
+    # State S: (K, V) — 128x128 fp32 = 64KB, fits in shared memory.
+
+    # Compute base pointers for this (b, h)
+    q_base = q_ptr + b_id * stride_q_b + h_id * stride_q_h
+    kd_base = kd_ptr + b_id * stride_kd_b + h_id * stride_kd_h
+    v_base = v_ptr + b_id * stride_v_b + h_id * stride_v_h
+    gl_base = g_last_ptr + b_id * stride_gl_b + h_id * stride_gl_h
+    w_base = w_ptr + b_id * stride_w_b + h_id * stride_w_h
+    u_base = u_ptr + b_id * stride_u_b + h_id * stride_u_h
+    kg_base = kg_ptr + b_id * stride_kg_b + h_id * stride_kg_h
+    o_base = o_ptr + b_id * stride_o_b + h_id * stride_o_h
+
+    # We need to hold S (K, V) in memory. Use shared memory.
+    # K = 128, V = 128: S has 128*128 = 16384 floats = 64KB.
+    # SM120 has 228KB shared memory per block → fits.
+
+    # But we also need to store the current chunk's data.
+    # The real question: can we process 128x128 S with tl.dot efficiently?
+    # We'll tile the operations.
+
+    # For now, we use a simpler approach: process one output row at a time,
+    # accumulating across tiles.
+
+    # --- Load state S from global memory into shared memory ---
+    # Actually, let's use a different strategy. We'll process S in tiles.
+    # S is K x V = 128 x 128.
+    # We tile K into KTILE chunks and V into VTILE chunks.
+
+    # Let's use KTILE=64, VTILE=64, and hold one tile of S in registers.
+    # We loop over the K and V dimensions.
+
+    # For each chunk step, we need to:
+    # 1. Load chunk data
+    # 2. Compute Aqk
+    # 3. Compute wS = w @ S
+    # 4. Compute qS = q @ S
+    # 5. Compute Aqk @ v_corrected
+    # 6. Compute state update kg^T @ v_corrected
+
+    # This is complex. Let me use a different decomposition.
+    pass
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Efficient PyTorch implementation for intra-chunk
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+
+def _intra_chunk(q, k, v, g, beta, scale, chunk_size):
+    """
+    Compute intra-chunk transition matrix A, w = A @ (exp(g)*k), u = A @ v.
+
+    All inputs in chunk form: (B, H, NT, BT, K) or (B, H, NT, BT, V) or (B, H, NT, BT).
+
+    Returns w, u, and auxiliary tensors needed for inter-chunk recurrence.
+    """
+    B, H, NT, BT, K = q.shape
+    V = v.shape[-1]
+    device = q.device
+    dtype = v.dtype
+
+    # Convert to fp32 for computation
+    q_f = q.float()
+    k_f = k.float()
+    v_f = v.float()
+    g_f = g.float()
+    beta_f = beta.float()
+
+    # Scale q
+    q_f = q_f * scale
+
+    # Cumsum on g along chunk time dimension
+    g_f = g_f.cumsum(-2)  # (B, H, NT, BT, K)
+
+    # Pre-compute exp(g) and exp(-g) for efficient matmuls
+    exp_g = g_f.exp()        # (B, H, NT, BT, K)
+    exp_neg_g = (-g_f).exp()  # (B, H, NT, BT, K)
+
+    # M_raw = (k * exp(g)) @ (k * exp(-g))^T scaled by beta per row
+    k_exp_g = k_f * exp_g      # (B, H, NT, BT, K)
+    k_exp_neg_g = k_f * exp_neg_g  # (B, H, NT, BT, K)
+
+    # M_raw: (B, H, NT, BT, BT)
+    # M_raw[b,h,n,i,j] = sum_d k_exp_g[b,h,n,i,d] * k_exp_neg_g[b,h,n,j,d]
+    M_raw = torch.einsum("... n i d, ... n j d -> ... n i j", k_exp_g, k_exp_neg_g)
+    M_raw = M_raw * beta_f.unsqueeze(-1).uns

import torch
import time
from solution import _intra_chunk, _inter_chunk_pytorch
from einops import rearrange

torch.manual_seed(0)
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

# Shape 1: B=2, T=2048, H=8, K=128, V=128
B, T, H, K, V, BT = 2, 2048, 8, 128, 128, 64
scale = K ** -0.5

q = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
k = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
v = torch.randn(B, T, H, V, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
g = (torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=device) * 0.1 - 0.05)
beta = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(B, T, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device))

q_c = rearrange(q, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
k_c = rearrange(k, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
v_c = rearrange(v, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
g_c = rearrange(g, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
beta_c = rearrange(beta, 'b (n c) h -> b h n c', c=BT)

# Warmup
for _ in range(3):
    w, u, qe, kd, gl, kg = _intra_chunk(q_c, k_c, v_c, g_c, beta_c, scale, BT)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Time intra
t0 = time.perf_counter()
for _ in range(20):
    w, u, qe, kd, gl, kg = _intra_chunk(q_c, k_c, v_c, g_c, beta_c, scale, BT)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
t1 = time.perf_counter()
print(f'Intra-chunk: {(t1-t0)/20*1000:.2f} ms')

# Time inter
for _ in range(3):
    o_c = _inter_chunk_pytorch(qe, kd, v_c, w, u, gl, kg)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

t0 = time.perf_counter()
for _ in range(20):
    o_c = _inter_chunk_pytorch(qe, kd, v_c, w, u, gl, kg)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
t1 = time.perf_counter()
print(f'Inter-chunk: {(t1-t0)/20*1000:.2f} ms')

So intra-chunk takes ~1.34ms and inter-chunk takes ~2.35ms. Both need optimization. The intra-chunk has a Python loop (BT=64 iterations) for the recurrence. That's slow but can be optimized with PyTorch bmm. The inter-chunk has a Python loop over NT=32 chunks, with each iteration doing several bmm calls. This is the main bottleneck. Let me think about the best approach: 1. For intra-chunk: The 64-iteration recurrence loop is in Python and processes small matrices. I can speed this up by doing the matrix power computation more efficiently. Actually, the loop does 64 iterations, each doing an einsum of shape (batch, BT) × (batch, BT, i) → (batch, i). This is dominated by Python overhead and small kernel launches. Alternative: compute (I + L)^{-1} directly using LU decomposition or solve. But L is strictly lower triangular, so I can use forward substitution. Actually, I need to compute A = (I + L)^{-1} @ diag(beta), which is equivalent to solving (I + L) @ A = diag(beta). Since (I+L) is lower triangular, I can solve column by column using forward substitution. For a lower triangular system L @ X = B where L has unit diagonal: X[i,:] = B[i,:] - sum_{j < i} L[i,j] * X[j,:] This is exactly what the recurrence computes! So I can't avoid the sequential computation over i. But I can do it more efficiently by noting that each step only depends on previous rows, and I can compute contributions incrementally. Actually, the recurrence as written in the reference is already O(BT^3), which is fine (64^3 = 262K per chunk). The issue is the Python loop with small kernel launches. Let me try a different approach: use `torch.linalg.solve_triangular` to compute (I+L)^{-1} @ beta_diag. This would be one cuBLAS call instead of 64 small ones. 2. For intra-chunk optimization: - Use batched solve: `torch.linalg.solve_triangular(I+L, beta_diag_matrix)` to compute (I+L)^{-1} @ diag(beta) Actually, I need A = (I+L)^{-1} @ diag(beta_col), where the output is (batch, BT, BT). But cuBLAS's trsm can handle this: it solves L @ X = B where L is lower triangular with unit diagonal, B is (batch, BT, BT), and X is of the same shape. Let me try this approach. But wait, in the reference, first there's a row scaling of M_raw by beta, then the recurrence, then column scaling by beta. Let me make sure my formulation matches. My formulation: - M_raw_0 = k_exp_g @ k_exp_neg_g^T (no beta) - M_raw = M_raw_0 * beta_row (row scaling) - L = tril(M_raw, diagonal=-1) (lower triangular) - A = (I+L)^{-1} @ diag(beta_col) But the reference has row beta at the start AND column beta at the end. Let me check: does this give the same result as a single beta scaling? From the reference: 1. A = M_raw_0 * beta_row (call this step: A_init[i,j] = M_raw_0[i,j] * beta_row[i]) 2. A = -A.masked_fill(upper+diag, 0) → A = -L where L_ij = M_raw_0[i,j] * beta_row[i] for j < i 3. Recurrence on A → A_final = (I+L)^{-1} - I (with negative signs from -L) Actually, let me denote L_pos = positive lower triangular. A = -L_pos. Recurrence: A_new = -L_pos - (-L_pos)^2 + (-L_pos)^3 - ... = -(L_pos - L_pos^2 + L_pos^3 - ...) = -(I - (I+L_pos)^{-1}) = (I+L_pos)^{-1} - I 4. A_final_row = (A + I) * beta_col = (I+L_pos)^{-1} * diag(beta_col) So the final A = (I+L_pos)^{-1} @ diag(beta_col) where L_pos[i,j] = M_raw_0[i,j] * beta_row[i]. The row beta is absorbed into L, and the column beta is applied after. These are NOT the same as a single beta scaling. In my code, I compute: - M_raw_0 = k_exp_g @ k_exp_neg_g^T (no beta) - Then apply beta_row: M_raw = M_raw_0 * beta.unsqueeze(-1) - Then L = tril(M_raw, diag=-1) - Then A_init = -L - Recurrence... - Then A = (A_recurred + I) * beta.unsqueeze(1) (column beta) Let me verify my code is correct (since it already passes check.py). The issue is just performance. For performance of intra-chunk: 1. The einsum for M_raw: M_raw = einsum("... n i d, ... n j d -> ... n i j", k_exp_g, k_exp_neg_g) This is batch * NT matmuls of size (BT × K) @ (K × BT). I can use bmm instead. 2. The recurrence loop (BT=64 iterations of Python-looping with small einsum calls) is slow. For #2, I can try: a) Use torch.linalg.solve_triangular b) Write a Triton kernel for the forward substitution c) Make the Python loop more efficient Let me try approach (a) first. I need to verify that solve_triangular gives the same result. Actually, let me think about this more carefully. I need to compute (I+L)^{-1} where L is strictly lower triangular. This is equivalent to solving (I+L) @ X = I for X, then X @ diag(beta_col). Using solve_triangular: - X = torch.linalg.solve_triangular(I+L, I, upper=False, unitriangular=True) - A = X @ diag(beta_col) = X * beta_col.unsqueeze(1) # column scaling But actually, I want A directly. solve_triangular supports batched solving with multiple RHS. import torch
torch.manual_seed(42)
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

# Test that solve_triangular gives the same result as the recurrence
BT = 8
batch = 4

# Create random L (strictly lower triangular, positive)
L = torch.rand(batch, BT, BT, device=device)
L = torch.tril(L, diagonal=-1)

# Create random beta
beta = torch.rand(batch, BT, device=device)

# Method 1: recurrence (like reference)
M = -L.clone()
mask = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, dtype=torch.bool, device=device), diagonal=0)
M = M.masked_fill(mask, 0)
for i in range(1, BT):
    A_i_all = M[:, i, :]
    A_all_i = M[:, :, :i]
    contrib = torch.einsum('b m, b m j -> b j', A_i_all, A_all_i)
    M[:, i, :i] = M[:, i, :i] + contrib
A_ref = (M + torch.eye(BT, device=device).unsqueeze(0)) * beta.unsqueeze(1)

# Method 2: solve_triangular
# (I - A0) = I + L (since A0 = -L)
# Solve (I - A0) @ X = diag(beta)
I_plus_L = torch.eye(BT, device=device).unsqueeze(0) + L
diag_beta = torch.diag_embed(beta)
A_solve = torch.linalg.solve_triangular(I_plus_L, diag_beta, upper=False, unitriangular=True)

diff = (A_ref - A_solve).abs().max().item()
print(f'Max diff between recurrence and solve_triangular: {diff:.2e}')
print(f'Match: {diff < 1e-5}')

import torch
from einops import rearrange
torch.manual_seed(0)
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

B, T, H, K, V, BT = 2, 1024, 8, 128, 128, 64
NT = T // BT
scale = K ** -0.5

q = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
k = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
v = torch.randn(B, T, H, V, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
g = (torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=device) * 0.1 - 0.05)
beta = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(B, T, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device))

q_c = rearrange(q, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT).float() * scale
k_c = rearrange(k, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT).float()
v_c = rearrange(v, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT).float()
g_c = rearrange(g, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT).float().cumsum(-2)
beta_c = rearrange(beta, 'b (n c) h -> b h n c', c=BT).float()

exp_g = g_c.exp()
exp_neg_g = (-g_c).exp()
k_exp_g = k_c * exp_g
k_exp_neg_g = k_c * exp_neg_g

# Old method: recurrence
M_raw = torch.einsum('b h n i d, b h n j d -> b h n i j', k_exp_g, k_exp_neg_g)
M_raw = M_raw * beta_c.unsqueeze(-1)  # row scaling
L_old = torch.tril(M_raw, diagonal=-1)

flat_batch = B * H * NT
L_flat = L_old.reshape(flat_batch, BT, BT)
beta_flat = beta_c.reshape(flat_batch, BT)

A_flat = -L_flat.clone()
mask = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, dtype=torch.bool, device=device), diagonal=0)
A_flat = A_flat.masked_fill(mask, 0)
for i in range(1, BT):
    contrib = torch.einsum('b m, b m j -> b j', A_flat[:, i, :], A_flat[:, :, :i])
    A_flat[:, i, :i] = A_flat[:, i, :i] + contrib
A_old_flat = (A_flat + torch.eye(BT, device=device).unsqueeze(0)) * beta_flat.unsqueeze(1)
A_old = A_old_flat.reshape(B, H, NT, BT, BT)

# New method: solve_triangular
# I + L (where L is lower triangular with positive entries)
# L = tril(M_raw, diag=-1) = tril(k_exp_g @ k_exp_neg_g^T * beta_row, diag=-1)
I_plus_L = torch.eye(BT, device=device).unsqueeze(0) + L_flat
diag_beta = torch.diag_embed(beta_flat)
A_new_flat = torch.linalg.solve_triangular(I_plus_L, diag_beta, upper=False, unitriangular=True)
A_new = A_new_flat.reshape(B, H, NT, BT, BT)

diff = (A_old - A_new).abs().max().item()
print(f'Max diff old vs new A: {diff:.2e}')

# Also verify w and u
k_exp_g_flat = k_exp_g.reshape(flat_batch, BT, K)
w_old = torch.bmm(A_old_flat, k_exp_g_flat).reshape(B, H, NT, BT, K)
w_new = torch.bmm(A_new_flat, k_exp_g_flat).reshape(B, H, NT, BT, K)
print(f'Max diff w: {(w_old - w_new).abs().max().item():.2e}')

v_flat = v_c.reshape(flat_batch, BT, V)
u_old = torch.bmm(A_old_flat, v_flat).reshape(B, H, NT, BT, V)
u_new = torch.bmm(A_new_flat, v_flat).reshape(B, H, NT, BT, V)
print(f'Max diff u: {(u_old - u_new).abs().max().item():.2e}')

Improved from 0.0013 to 0.0029 — better but still terrible. The inter-chunk loop with 32+ Python iterations is the bottleneck. I need to write a proper Triton kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence. Let me implement the fused Triton kernel now. The key insight: each (B, H) pair processes NT chunks sequentially. But all (B, H) pairs are independent. So I can have one block per (B, H) pair, and each block loops over chunks. For each chunk step, the block needs to: 1. Compute Aqk = tril(q @ kd^T) — (BT, BT) 2. Compute wS = w @ S — (BT, V) 3. Compute qS = q @ S — (BT, V) 4. Compute vc = u - wS 5. Compute Aqk_vc = Aqk @ vc — (BT, V) 6. Write output: o_n = qS + Aqk_vc 7. Update S: S = S * gl + kg^T @ vc — (K, V) These are all matrix multiplications: - q @ kd^T: (BT, K) @ (K, BT) → (BT, BT) - w @ S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) → (BT, V) - q @ S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) → (BT, V) - Aqk @ vc: (BT, BT) @ (BT, V) → (BT, V) - kg^T @ vc: (K, BT) @ (BT, V) → (K, V) With BT=64, K=128, V=128: - The largest matmul is (128, 64) @ (64, 128) → (128, 128) = state update - And (64, 128) @ (128, 128) → (64, 128) = q@S, w@S For each matmul, with tl.dot tile size 16×16: - (64, 128) @ (128, 128): 4×8 output tiles, 8 reduction tiles → 256 MMA ops - (64, 128) @ (128, 64): 4×4 output tiles, 8 reduction tiles → 128 MMA ops - (128, 64) @ (64, 128): 8×8 output tiles, 4 reduction tiles → 256 MMA ops Each MMA op is a 16×16×16 matmul = 8K FMA operations. Total per chunk step: - Aqk: 128 * 8K = 1M FMA - w@S: 256 * 8K = 2M FMA - q@S: 256 * 8K = 2M FMA - Aqk@vc: 256 * 8K = 2M FMA - kg^T@vc: 256 * 8K = 2M FMA - Total: 9M FMA per chunk per (B,H) For B=2, H=8, NT=32: 9M * 16 * 32 = 4.6G FMA = 9.2G FLOPs. On 90 TFLOPS, pure compute time = 9.2G / 90T = 0.1 microseconds? No, 9.2G/90T = 0.0001s = 100 microseconds. That's still tiny. The key optimization is eliminating launch overhead. A single kernel launch for all chunks per (B,H) eliminates the NT × (multiple bmm) launch overhead. Let me write the Triton kernel now. I need to be careful about the tiling and data management. Let me design the kernel: Grid: (B * H,) — one block per (B, H) pair Block size: 128 threads (4 warps) Each block: 1. Initializes state S (K×V) in shared memory to zero 2. For each chunk n = 0..NT-1: a. Load chunk data (q, kd, v, w, u, gl, kg) — either all at once or tile by tile b. Compute Aqk, wS, qS, vc, Aqk_vc, output c. Update S 3. Store output The challenge is fitting all the chunk data in shared memory. Each chunk has: - q: 64×128 = 8K fp32 = 32KB - kd: 64×128 = 32KB - v: 64×128 = 32KB - w: 64×128 = 32KB - u: 64×128 = 32KB - kg: 64×128 = 32KB - gl: 128 = 0.5KB - S: 128×128 = 16K fp32 = 64KB Total: 256KB+. Shared memory limit is 228KB. So I can't fit everything at once. Strategy: Load data tile by tile from global memory. Use pipelining. Actually, let me think more carefully. The matmuls are small. What if I: 1. Keep S in shared memory (64KB) 2. Load other data as needed for each matmul 3. Store intermediate results in shared memory temporarily For example, to compute w@S: - Load w tile by tile from global memory - For each tile, compute w_tile @ S using tl.dot - Accumulate in output buffer To compute Aqk: - Load q and kd tile by tile - Compute q_tile @ kd_tile^T using tl.dot - Apply mask and store Let me write this kernel. I'll use a simpler approach where each block uses 128 threads to compute matmuls cooperatively. The key is that the matrices are small enough that we don't need complex tiling. Actually, for a 64×128 × 128×64 matmul, the output is 64×64 = 4096 elements. With 128 threads, each thread handles 32 output elements. Each output element requires 128 FMA operations. That's 4096*128 = 524K operations per matmul. For the inter-chunk, we do 5 matmuls per chunk × 32 chunks = 160 matmuls. That's 84M operations, which should take < 1 microsecond on 90 TFLOPS. So the bottleneck is entirely in memory access and launch overhead, not compute. Given this, let me write the kernel to minimize global memory access. I'll load each chunk's data once and reuse it. For the inter-chunk kernel, the key data structures per chunk are: - q: (BT, K) = 64×128 = 8K fp32 - kd: (BT, K) = 8K fp32 - v: (BT, V) = 64×128 = 8K fp32 - w: (BT, K) = 8K fp32 - u: (BT, V) = 8K fp32 - kg: (BT, K) = 8K fp32 - gl: (K,) = 128 fp32 Total input per chunk: ~48K fp32 = 192KB. Plus S: 64KB. Total: 256KB. Too much for shared memory. But we can load them in stages: - Stage 1: Load q, kd → compute Aqk → free q, kd - Stage 2: Load w → compute w@S → free w - Stage 3: Load v → compute vc = u - wS → keep vc - Stage 4: Load q → compute q@S → free q - Stage 5: Aqk @ vc → compute, add to output - Stage 6: Load kg → kg^T @ vc → update S But this requires many loads from global memory. Still slow at 0.0037 peak fraction. The inter-chunk loop with Python iteration over chunks and individual bmm calls per chunk is the bottleneck. I really need to write a proper fused Triton kernel for the inter-chunk recurrence. Let me think about the most efficient approach for the Triton kernel. The key operations are all matmuls involving state S which is K×V=128×128. S must be maintained in shared memory and accessed efficiently. Let me design the kernel more carefully: **Grid layout:** One block per (B*H) pair. Within each block, we process all NT chunks sequentially. **Shared memory:** - State S: K×V = 128×128 fp32 = 64KB. Tile it for efficient access. - Scratch buffers: temporary storage for intermediate results. **Thread configuration:** - 128 threads (4 warps) - Use warp-level cooperative matrix operations **Operations per chunk:** 1. w@S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) → (BT, V) 2. q@S: (BT, K) @ (K, V) → (BT, V) 3. Aqk@vc: (BT, BT) @ (BT, V) → (BT, V) 4. kg^T@vc: (K, BT) @ (BT, V) → (K, V) All four are matmuls. With BT=64, K=128, V=128, these are small enough to be done with straightforward tiling. For w@S: tile BT→4 tiles of 16, V→8 tiles of 16, K→8 tiles of 16. Total: 4×8×8 = 256 tl.dot calls per chunk. For q@S: same as w@S, 256 calls. For Aqk@vc: tile BT→4 of 16, V→8 of 16, BT (reduce)→4 of 16. Total: 4×8×4 = 128 calls. For kg^T@vc: tile K→8 of 16, V→8 of 16, BT (reduce)→4 of 16. Total: 8×8×4 = 256 calls. Total per chunk: ~896 tl.dot calls. For NT=32, that's ~28,672 tl.dot calls per block. Each tl.dot call does a 16×16×16 = 4096 fused multiply-adds = 4096 operations. Total per (B,H) block: 28,672 × 4096 ≈ 117M FMA ops, or 234M FLOPs. At the warp level, with 4 warps, each warp handles 1/4 of the tiles = ~7168 tl.dot calls. Each 16×16×16 MMA takes ~8 cycles on Blackwell. So ~57K cycles per warp. At ~2GHz, that's ~28 microseconds. So the compute time is actually tiny. The bottleneck is memory access. With 64KB of S in shared memory and tiled loading from global memory, memory access should be efficient. The main performance consideration is: 1. Loading data from global memory efficiently (coalesced loads) 2. Minimizing synchronization between warps 3. Hiding memory latency with async copy (cp.async on SM80+) Let me write the kernel now. I'll focus on making it correct first, then optimize. Actually, a key design decision: how to handle the 4 different matmuls efficiently. They all involve S, so S should stay in shared memory. For the other operands, I can load them tile by tile from global memory. For w@S and q@S, the RHS is S (K×V). The LHS is w or q (BT×K). I'll load tiles of w/q from global memory and tiles of S from shared memory. For Aqk@vc, the LHS is Aqk (BT×BT, pre-computed) and RHS is vc (BT×V, freshly computed). I can keep vc in shared memory after computing it. For kg^T@vc, the LHS is kg^T (K×BT) and RHS is vc (BT×V). This updates S. Let me restructure the computation: 1. Compute wS = w @ S → store in shared memory as wS (BT×V) 2. Compute vc = u - wS → modify wS in-place to get vc 3. Compute qS = q @ S → store in shared memory as qS (BT×V) 4. Compute Aqk_vc = Aqk @ vc → accumulate with qS, write to output 5. Compute kg^T @ vc → update S Shared memory budget (228KB on SM120): - S: 128×128 fp32 = 64KB - vc: 64×128 fp32 = 32KB - qS/scratch: 64×128 fp32 = 32KB - Total: 128KB. Fits! But I can share the scratch buffer: use the same memory for wS, vc, qS (all are BT×V). Let me now write the kernel. I'll use a block of 128 threads, partitioned into 4 warps. For each chunk: 1. Warps 0,1 compute w@S (split the BT dimension), storing in vc buffer 2. Warps 0,1 subtract vc = u - wS (elementwise) 3. Warps 2,3 compute q@S (split the BT dimension), storing in qS buffer 4. All warps compute Aqk@vc, accumulating with qS for output 5. All warps compute kg^T@vc, updating S Actually, a cleaner approach: use all 128 threads cooperatively for each matmul. Each matmul has output tiles that can be distributed among threads/warps. For a 64×128 output, with tile size 16×16, there are 4×8 = 32 output tiles. With 4 warps, each warp handles 8 tiles. OK, let me just write the kernel. I'll use a relatively straightforward approach. Let me think about how to structure the Triton code. Triton programs are per-thread, not per-warp. But tl.dot uses warp-level matrix multiply under the hood. So for efficient use of tl.dot: - Each Triton program (thread) in a block participates in the cooperative tl.dot - I need to iterate over tiles and have all threads participate Let me use a single-program approach where each program computes one element of the output. With 128 threads and output size up to 128×128=16384, each thread handles 128 elements. Not ideal for tl.dot. Better: use 2D grid within a block. Process output tiles with groups of threads. Actually, the most practical approach for small matrices is to use a sequential approach where all threads cooperate on each tile. Something like: ``` for m in range(0, BT, TM): for n in range(0, V, TN): acc = zeros(TM, TN) for k_idx in range(0, K, TK): a = load(w[m:m+TM, k_idx:k_idx+TK]) b = load(S[k_idx:k_idx+TK, n:n+TN]) acc += tl.dot(a, b) store(acc, output[m:m+TM, n:n+TN]) ``` This is straightforward to write in Triton but may not be the most efficient. Let me try it. Actually, for a block with 128 threads, I should use all threads to load data and compute. In Triton, I can use `tl.program_id` within a 1D grid but still have all threads participate in tile operations. Let me use this approach: each thread computes one output element per tile. With TM=16 and 128 threads per block, I can compute up to 128 elements per tile. For a 16×16 tile (=256 elements), with 128 threads, each thread handles 2 elements. That's efficient. But Triton's tl.dot doesn't work this way — it auto-distributes the work among threads. So I just need to write the loops and let Triton handle the distribution. Let me write a working kernel now. I'll use simple tiling with all threads participating in each tl.dot operation. import torch
import time
from einops import rearrange
torch.manual_seed(0)
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

B, T, H, K, V, BT = 2, 2048, 8, 128, 128, 64
NT = T // BT
scale = K ** -0.5

def inter_chunk_compiled(q_exp_g, k_exp_neg_g, w, u, exp_g_last, kg):
    BH, NT2, BT2, K2 = q_exp_g.shape
    V2 = u.shape[-1]
    
    # Pre-compute Aqk
    qe_all = q_exp_g.reshape(BH * NT2, BT2, K2)
    kd_all = k_exp_neg_g.reshape(BH * NT2, BT2, K2)
    Aqk_all = torch.bmm(qe_all, kd_all.transpose(1, 2))
    mask = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT2, BT2, dtype=torch.bool, device=q_exp_g.device), diagonal=1)
    Aqk_all.masked_fill_(mask, 0)
    Aqk_flat = Aqk_all.reshape(BH, NT2, BT2, BT2)
    
    S = torch.zeros(BH, K2, V2, dtype=torch.float32, device=q_exp_g.device)
    o_flat = torch.empty(BH, NT2, BT2, V2, dtype=torch.float32, device=q_exp_g.device)
    
    for n in range(NT2):
        q_n = q_exp_g[:, n]
        w_n = w[:, n]
        u_n = u[:, n]
        Aqk_n = Aqk_flat[:, n]
        gl_n = exp_g_last[:, n]
        kg_n = kg[:, n]
        
        wS = torch.bmm(w_n, S)
        vc = u_n - wS
        qS = torch.bmm(q_n, S)
        Aqk_vc = torch.bmm(Aqk_n, vc)
        o_flat[:, n] = qS + Aqk_vc
        
        S = S * gl_n.unsqueeze(-1)
        S_update = torch.bmm(kg_n.transpose(1, 2), vc)
        S = S + S_update
    
    return o_flat

# Create test inputs
q = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
k = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
v = torch.randn(B, T, H, V, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
g = (torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=device) * 0.1 - 0.05)
beta = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(B, T, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device))

from solution import _intra_chunk_fast
q_c = rearrange(q, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
k_c = rearrange(k, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
v_c = rearrange(v, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
g_c = rearrange(g, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
beta_c = rearrange(beta, 'b (n c) h -> b h n c', c=BT)

w, u, q_exp_g, k_exp_neg_g, exp_g_last, kg = _intra_chunk_fast(q_c, k_c, v_c, g_c, beta_c, scale)

# Flatten B,H
BH = B * H
qe_f = q_exp_g.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)
kd_f = k_exp_neg_g.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)
w_f = w.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)
u_f = u.reshape(BH, NT, BT, V)
gl_f = exp_g_last.reshape(BH, NT, K)
kg_f = kg.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)

# Warmup
_ = inter_chunk_compiled(qe_f, kd_f, w_f, u_f, gl_f, kg_f)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

# Time uncompiled
t0 = time.perf_counter()
for _ in range(20):
    _ = inter_chunk_compiled(qe_f, kd_f, w_f, u_f, gl_f, kg_f)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
t1 = time.perf_counter()
print(f'Uncompiled: {(t1-t0)/20*1000:.3f} ms')

# Compile
print('Compiling...')
compiled_fn = torch.compile(inter_chunk_compiled, mode='reduce-overhead', fullgraph=False)
_ = compiled_fn(qe_f, kd_f, w_f, u_f, gl_f, kg_f)
torch.cuda.synchronize()

t0 = time.perf_counter()
for _ in range(20):
    _ = compiled_fn(qe_f, kd_f, w_f, u_f, gl_f, kg_f)
torch.cuda.synchronize()
t1 = time.perf_counter()
print(f'Compiled (reduce-overhead): {(t1-t0)/20*1000:.3f} ms')

# Try max-autotune
print('Compiling (max-autotune)...')
compiled_fn2 = torch.compile(inter_chunk_compiled, mode='max-autotune', fullgraph=False)
_ = compiled_fn2(qe_f, kd_f, w_f, u_f, gl_f, kg_f)

+def _intra_chunk_fast(q, k, v, g, beta, scale):

+
+    Uses batched bmm + solve_triangular to avoid the 64-step Python recurrence.

-    # We tile S: store V_TILE columns at a time, loading K rows
-    # Since K=128, V=128, and BT_TILE=32, V_TILE=32:
-    #   S tiles: 4 K-tiles x 4 V-tiles = 16 tiles, each 32x32 = 1K floats = 4KB
-    # We need to access S for w@S (BTxK @ KxV), q@S (BTxK @ KxV), and S update (KxBT @ BTxV)
-
-    # Strategy: process output tiles of (BT_TILE, V_TILE). For each output tile,
-    # iterate over K reduction tiles. Load S tiles as needed.
-
-    # Since this is complex, we use a simpler approach:
-    # Process one BT_TILE row block at a time, computing all operations for that block.

-    q_ptr, w_ptr, u_ptr, Aqk_ptr, kg_ptr, gl_ptr, o_ptr,

-    """
-    Simplified fused inter-chunk kernel — one block per (B,H), serial over chunks.
-
-    Uses 128-thread cooperative approach:
-    - State S (KxV) stored in shared memory as K rows of V floats
-    - Each chunk's operations: w@S, q@S, Aqk@vc, state update
-    - Use tl.dot with tile sizes tuned for small matrices (16x16 tiles)
-
-    Memory layout:
-    - S: K rows x V cols in shared memory [K * V floats = 64KB for K=128,V=128]
-    - Scratch: buffer for intermediate results
-
-    Since we need S for multiple tl.dot calls (w@S and q@S share S as the RHS),
-    we need S in a form that tl.dot can use. tl.dot requires inputs as 2D arrays.
-
-    Plan: for each operation, tile the computation:
-    1. w@S: tile BT into rows, V into cols. For each tile (bt_tile x v_tile):
-       - Load w[bt_start:bt_end, :] from global
-       - Load S[:, v_start:v_end] from shared mem (strip-mine K)
-       - Accumulate via tl.dot(w_tile_k, S_tile_kv) in a loop over K tiles
-    2. Similarly for q@S
-    3. Aqk@vc: tile BT, V, accumulate over BT
-    4. kg^T@vc: tile K, V, accumulate over BT
-
-    All accumulation happens in registers, result written to output.
-    """
-    bh_id = tl.program_id(0)
-
-    # Pointers
-    q_bh   = q_ptr   + bh_id * stride_bh_q
-    w_bh   = w_ptr   + bh_id * stride_bh_w
-    u_bh   = u_ptr   + bh_id * stride_bh_u
-    aqk_bh = Aqk_ptr + bh_id * stride_bh_aqk
-    kg_bh  = kg_ptr  + bh_id * stride_bh_kg
-    gl_bh  = gl_ptr  + bh_id * stride_bh_gl
-    o_bh   = o_ptr   + bh_id * stride_bh_o
-
-    # Tile sizes
-    TM: tl.constexpr = 32  # rows of output (BT) per tile
-    TN: tl.constexpr = 32  # cols of output (V) per tile
-    TK: tl.constexpr = 16  # reduction dim (K) per tile
-
-    NUM_TM = tl.cdiv(BT, TM)
-    NUM_TN = tl.cdiv(V, TN)
-    NUM_TK = tl.cdiv(K, TK)
-
-    # Thread mapping
-    pid_bt = tl.program_id(1)  # BT tile id
-    pid_v = tl.program_id(2)   # V tile id

import torch, time
from einops import rearrange
from solution import _intra_chunk_fast, _inter_chunk_fn

torch.manual_seed(0)
device = torch.device('cuda:0')

for shape_name, (B, T, H, K, V) in [
    ('shape0', (2, 1024, 8, 128, 128)),
    ('shape1', (2, 2048, 8, 128, 128)),
    ('shape2', (1, 4096, 8, 128, 128)),
    ('shape3', (1, 2048, 4, 128, 128)),
]:
    BT, scale = 64, K ** -0.5
    NT = T // BT
    BH = B * H
    
    q = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
    k = torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
    v = torch.randn(B, T, H, V, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device) * 0.1
    g = (torch.randn(B, T, H, K, dtype=torch.float32, device=device) * 0.1 - 0.05)
    beta = torch.sigmoid(torch.randn(B, T, H, dtype=torch.bfloat16, device=device))
    
    q_c = rearrange(q, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
    k_c = rearrange(k, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
    v_c = rearrange(v, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
    g_c = rearrange(g, 'b (n c) h d -> b h n c d', c=BT)
    beta_c = rearrange(beta, 'b (n c) h -> b h n c', c=BT)
    
    # Warmup
    w, u, qe, kd, gl, kg = _intra_chunk_fast(q_c, k_c, v_c, g_c, beta_c, scale)
    qe_f = qe.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)
    kd_f = kd.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)
    w_f = w.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)
    u_f = u.reshape(BH, NT, BT, V)
    gl_f = gl.reshape(BH, NT, K)
    kg_f = kg.reshape(BH, NT, BT, K)
    mask = torch.triu(torch.ones(BT, BT, dtype=torch.bool, device=device), diagonal=1)
    
    # Intra timing
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    t0 = time.perf_counter()
    for _ in range(30):
        w, u, qe, kd, gl, kg = _intra_chunk_fast(q_c, k_c, v_c, g_c, beta_c, scale)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    t_intra = (time.perf_counter() - t0) / 30 * 1000
    
    # Inter timing (uncompiled)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    t0 = time.perf_counter()
    for _ in range(30):
        _inter_chunk_fn(qe_f, kd_f, w_f, u_f, gl_f, kg_f, mask)
    torch.cuda.synchronize()
    t_inter = (time.perf_counter() - t0) / 30 * 1000
    
    # Total theoretical flops
    flops = 4 * B * T * H * (K*V + BT*K + BT*V)
    
    print(f'{shape_name} B={B} T={T} H={H} NT={NT}: intra={t_intra:.3f}ms inter={t_inter:.3f}ms total={t_intra+t_inter:.3f}ms flops={flops/1e9:.2f}G')
" 2>&1

+    cuda_src = r"""
+#include <cuda_fp16.h>
+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+
+// SM120 (Blackwell) warp matrix multiply using mma.sync
+// For the inter-chunk recurrence, we need small matmuls:
+//   w@S:  (64,128) @ (128,128) -> (64,128)
+//   q@S:  (64,128) @ (128,128) -> (64,128)
+//   Aqk@vc: (64,64) @ (64,128) -> (64,128)
+//   kg^T@vc: (128,64) @ (64,128) -> (128,128)
+//
+// We use warp-cooperative computation with shared-memory state S.
+
+#define BT 64
+#define K_DIM 128
+#define V_DIM 128
+
+#define WARP_SIZE 32
+#define WARPS_PER_BLOCK 4
+#define THREADS_PER_BLOCK (WARP_SIZE * WARPS_PER_BLOCK)
+
+// Tile sizes for warp-level matmul
+#define TM 16
+#define TN 16
+#define TK 16
+
+// Helper: warp-level bf16 matmul (C = A @ B) using __mma operations.
+// On SM120, we can use __hmma_mma_sync or the older wmma interface.
+// For simplicity and portability, we use a direct FMA loop (not ideal but correct).
+// TODO: replace with actual mma.sync for performance.
+
+__global__ void inter_chunk_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ qe,      // (BH, NT, BT, K)
+    const float* __restrict__ w,       // (BH, NT, BT, K)
+    const float* __restrict__ u,       // (BH, NT, BT, V)
+    const float* __restrict__ Aqk,     // (BH, NT, BT, BT)
+    const float* __restrict__ kg,      // (BH, NT, BT, K)
+    const float* __restrict__ gl,      // (BH, NT, K)
+    float* __restrict__ o,             // (BH, NT, BT, V)
+    int BH,
+    int NT
+) {
+    int bh = blockIdx.x;
+    if (bh >= BH) return;
+
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    int warp_id = tid / WARP_SIZE;
+    int lane_id = tid % WARP_SIZE;
+
+    // Strides
+    int stride_qe  = NT * BT * K_DIM;
+    int stride_w   = NT * BT * K_DIM;
+    int stride_u   = NT * BT * V_DIM;
+    int stride_aqk = NT * BT * BT;
+    int stride_kg  = NT * BT * K_DIM;
+    int stride_gl  = NT * K_DIM;
+    int stride_o   = NT * BT * V_DIM;
+
+    // Base pointers for this bh
+    const float* qe_bh  = qe  + bh * stride_qe;
+    const float* w_bh   = w   + bh * stride_w;
+    const float* u_bh   = u   + bh * stride_u;
+    const float* aqk_bh = Aqk + bh * stride_aqk;
+    const float* kg_bh  = kg  + bh * stride_kg;
+    const float* gl_bh  = gl  + bh * stride_gl;
+    float* o_bh         = o   + bh * stride_o;
+
+    // State S: (K_DIM, V_DIM) in shared memory
+    __shared__ float S_smem[K_DIM * V_DIM];
+
+    // Initialize S to zero
+    for (int i = tid; i < K_DIM * V_DIM; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) {
+        S_smem[i] = 0.0f;
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    // Process chunks sequentially
+    for (int n = 0; n < NT; n++) {
+        const float* qe_n  = qe_bh  + n * BT * K_DIM;
+        const float* w_n   = w_bh   + n * BT * K_DIM;
+        const float* u_n   = u_bh   + n * BT * V_DIM;

+        const float* kg_n  = kg_bh  + n * BT * K_DIM;
+        const float* gl_n  = gl_bh  + n * K_DIM;
+        float* o_n         = o_bh   + n * BT * V_DIM;
+
+        // ---- Step 1: wS = w_n @ S_smem ----
+        // w_n: (BT, K_DIM), S_smem: (K_DIM, V_DIM) -> wS: (BT, V_DIM)
+        // Each thread computes some elements of wS
+
+        // wS stored in shared memory temporarily: (BT, V_DIM)
+        __shared__ float ws_buf[BT * V_DIM];
+
+        // Initialize wS buffer
+        for (int i = tid; i < BT * V_DIM; i += THREADS_PER_BLOCK) {
+            ws_buf[i] = 0.0f;
+        }
+        __sync

-    Correctness-first inter-chunk kernel.
-
-    Process one (B,H) per block. Each block has BLOCK_SIZE threads.
-    Process chunks one at a time.
-
-    For each chunk:

I should restructure to avoid the `if` and use the mask parameter of `tl.load` and `tl.store` instead, or use `tl.where`. Let me restructure the kernel to be more Triton-idiomatic. For each phase, instead of: ```python if active: acc += tl.load(...) ``` I should do: ```python acc += tl.load(...) # The load returns 0 for out-of-bounds with mask ``` But actually, the simpler fix is to not use `if active:` at all and just do the loads unconditionally. For out-of-bounds indices, the loads will be predicated. But for loops over `range(K)`, all threads participate. Wait, the issue is that inner loop `for kk in range(K)` should only execute for active threads. But in Triton, `for kk in range(K)` is a Python-level loop that's unrolled at compile time. All threads in the block execute it. For inactive threads, the loads should return 0. But for Phase 1, when `active = False`, we still execute the inner loop and do tl.load for bi and vj that are out of bounds! This will cause out-of-bounds memory access. The fix: always guard memory accesses with masks. Or use `tl.where` to conditionally add. In Triton, `tl.load(ptr, mask=mask)` returns 0 for masked-out elements. And `tl.store(ptr, val, mask=mask)` doesn't write for masked-out elements. So the fix is: ```python acc = 0.0 for kk in range(K): acc += tl.load(w_n + bi * K + kk, mask=active, other=0.0) * tl.load(S_bh + kk * V + vj, mask=active, other=0.0) tl.store(o_n + bi * V + vj, acc, mask=active) ``` But this is very wasteful — inactive threads still execute K iterations of loads (though they don't access memory due to the mask). An alternative: use `tl.where` for the entire accumulation: ```python acc = tl.where(active, computation, 0.0) ``` Actually, the cleanest approach for Triton is to restructure the loops so that each iteration only processes valid elements, using the `range` limits. The pattern I'm using (thread-parallelization over elements in a block) is common in Triton. Let me use the more idiomatic pattern: ```python for base in range(0, total_wS, BLOCK_SIZE): idx = tid + base bi = idx // V vj = idx % V active = idx < total_wS acc = 0.0 for kk in range(K): w_val = tl.load(w_n + bi * K + kk, mask=active, other=0.0) s_val = tl.load(S_bh + kk * V + vj, mask=active, other=0.0) acc += w_val * s_val tl.store(o_n + bi * V + vj, acc, mask=active) ``` This should work because: - For active threads: bi, vj are valid, loads work normally - For inactive threads: mask=False, loads return 0.0, acc stays 0.0, store doesn't happen The redundant computation (inactive threads still compute 0.0) is a waste but correct. But there's still a subtle issue with loops over `range(K)`. In Triton PTX compilation, these loops ARE unrolled (or kept as loops). The mask applies to the load/store instructions, not the loop itself. So inactive threads still execute the loop body but don't access memory. Actually, this is fine for correctness. The performance impact is that inactive threads waste cycles (they do the same loop iterations but don't access memory). This is standard GPU behavior. Let me rewrite the kernel.

I'm still using `tl.zeros([1])` and `tl.sum` which might cause issues. Let me simplify and just use scalar accumulators with `tl.where` for conditional accumulation. Actually, in Triton, if I have a scalar (like `acc = 0.0`), I can accumulate into it with `acc += tl.sum(tl.where(m, wv * sv, 0.0))` or similar. But the simplest correct pattern for this use case is: ```python acc = 0.0 for kk in range(K): wv = tl.load(w_n + bi * K + kk, mask=m, other=0.0) sv = tl.load(S_bh + kk * V + vj, mask=m, other=0.0) acc += tl.sum(tl.where(m, wv * sv, 0.0)) ``` Wait, `acc` is a scalar but `wv` and `sv` are tensors of size BLOCK_SIZE. So `wv * sv` is element-wise and `acc += tl.sum(...)` sums all elements and adds to the scalar. But this would sum across ALL threads, not just active ones. Actually, in Triton, `tl.load` with a mask returns a tensor of BLOCK_SIZE values. Each element corresponds to one thread's value. The `other=0.0` means masked-out elements are 0. When I do `wv * sv`, this is element-wise multiplication of BLOCK_SIZE values. When I do `acc += tl.sum(...)`, this sums ALL BLOCK_SIZE values. But I want each thread's accumulator to be independent! This is the fundamental issue with my approach. In Triton, each "program" (thread) is supposed to compute independently. The `tl.arange` pattern creates a SIMD-like vectorization. Let me reconsider. The standard Triton pattern is: 1. Each program (thread) computes one or more output elements 2. Threads are indexed by `tl.program_id(axis)` for grid-level parallelism, and within a block, threads are indexed by an axis too. But my kernel has a 1D grid (BH,) and within each block, I want threads to cooperate. The standard way is to use the grid dimensions to express thread-level parallelism: Grid: (BH, cdiv(total_work, BLOCK_SIZE)) Each program: compute BLOCK_SIZE elements This eliminates the tl.arange complexity. Let me restructure. Actually, the simplest correct approach is: - Use multiple kernel launches (one per chunk), with grid covering all (B,H) pairs - This avoids the inter-block synchronization problem - Each launch does all the matmuls for one chunk But this adds launch overhead per chunk... Wait, I made a mistake in the CUDA graph code. When S_tmp is assigned to with `S_tmp = S_tmp * gl...`, this creates a new tensor and the graph capture might not capture it correctly. In CUDA graph capture, all operations must have fixed memory addresses. The in-place `S_tmp = S_tmp * gl...` might allocate new memory. Let me fix this by using in-place operations: - `S_tmp.mul_(gl_f[:, n].unsqueeze(-1))` instead of `S_tmp = S_tmp * ...` - `S_tmp.add_(bmm_result)` instead of `S_tmp = S_tmp + ...` Similarly, `o_f[:, n] = ...` needs to be in-place. Actually, `o_f[:, n] = qS + Aqk_vc` is a slice assignment which copies. But the RHS creates a new tensor from `qS + Aqk_vc`. In CUDA graph mode, this should work since the operations are captured and the addresses are determined at capture time. But wait, `S_tmp = S_tmp * gl...` — this reassignment changes which tensor `S_tmp` points to. In the graph, the original S_tmp is captured, and the new assignment creates a new tensor. On replay, the original S_tmp is used for the input, and the new assignment is re-executed (creating another new tensor). This should work as long as the intermediate allocations are captured. Actually, CUDA graphs have strict requirements about memory allocation. No dynamic allocations during replay. All intermediate tensors must be pre-allocated. The `*` and `+` operations on tensors allocate new output tensors each time. This won't work in a CUDA graph. Let me fix this by using in-place operations and pre-allocating all intermediates. Actually wait, PyTorch's CUDA graph capture handles this. During capture, all memory allocations are recorded. During replay, the same memory is reused. So `S_tmp = S_tmp * gl...` should work in the graph because PyTorch's graph allocator manages the intermediate memory. Let me just check if this compiles and runs correctly first.

-"""KDA forward (chunk form) — optimized for SM120 Blackwell.

+"""KDA forward (chunk form) — Triton kernel with tl.dot for SM120 Blackwell.
+
+Architecture:
+  - Intra-chunk: batched bmm + solve_triangular (cuBLAS)
   - Pre-compute all-chunk Aqk in one batched bmm
-  - Inter-chunk recurrence: CUDA graph capturing all chunk iterations,
-    with fused w@S+q@S (stacked bmm) to minimize kernel launches.
-  - All matmuls use fp32 precision via cuBLAS (which uses Tensor Cores
-    for bf16→fp32 accumulation internally).
-
-The CUDA graph replay eliminates launch overhead for the sequential recurrence.
+  - Inter-chunk recurrence: single Triton kernel, one block per (B,H),
+    with state S in shared memory (64KB), all matmuls via tl.dot.
 """
 
 from __future__ import annotations
 
 import torch
 import torch.nn as nn
+import triton
+import triton.language as tl
 from einops import rearrange
 
 OP_TYPE = "linear_attention"
 SUPPORTED_PRECISIONS = ["bf16"]
 HARDWARE_REQUIRED = ["RTX_PRO_6000", "H100", "B200"]
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Triton kernel: matmul with shared-memory RHS
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+@triton.jit
+def _dot_lhs_smem(
+    lhs_ptr,          # global memory
+    rhs_smem_ptr,     # shared memory offset
+    acc,              # accumulator tile [TM, TN]
+    M_start, N_start, K_dim,
+    stride_lhs_m, stride_lhs_k,
+    stride_rhs_k, stride_rhs_n,
+    TM: tl.constexpr, TN: tl.constexpr, TK: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    """Compute acc += lhs[M_start:M_start+TM, :K_dim] @ rhs_smem[:K_dim, N_start:N_start+TN]"""
+    for k_start in range(0, K_dim, TK):
+        k_off = tl.arange(0, TK)
+        m_off = tl.arange(0, TM)
+        n_off = tl.arange(0, TN)
+
+        # Load lhs tile: (TM, TK)
+        lhs_ptrs = lhs_ptr + (m_off[:, None] + M_start) * stride_lhs_m + (k_off[None, :] + k_start) * stride_lhs_k
+        lhs_tile = tl.load(lhs_ptrs)
+
+        # Load rhs tile from shared memory: (TK, TN)
+        rhs_ptrs = rhs_smem_ptr + (k_off[:, None] + k_start) * stride_rhs_k + (n_off[None, :] + N_start) * stride_rhs_n
+        rhs_tile = tl.load(rhs_ptrs)
+
+        acc += tl.dot(lhs_tile, rhs_tile)
+
+
+@triton.jit
+def _dot_smem_rhs(
+    lhs_ptr,          # global memory
+    rhs_smem_ptr,     # shared memory offset
+    M_range, N_range, K_dim,
+    stride_lhs_m, stride_lhs_k,
+    stride_rhs_k, stride_rhs_n,
+    TM: tl.constexpr, TN: tl.constexpr, TK: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    """Compute C[M_range[0]:M_range[1], N_range[0]:N_range[1]] = lhs @ rhs_smem"""
+    M_start, M_end = M_range
+    N_start, N_end = N_range
+    acc = tl.zeros([TM, TN], dtype=tl.float32)
+
+    for k_start in range(0, K_dim, TK):
+        m_off = tl.arange(0, TM)
+        n_off = tl.arange(0, TN)
+        k_off = tl.arange(0, TK)
+
+        lhs_ptrs = lhs_ptr + (M_start + m_off[:, None]) * stride_lhs_m + (k_start + k_off[None, :]) * stride_lhs_k
+        lhs_tile = tl.load(lhs_ptrs)
+
+        rhs_ptrs = rhs_smem_ptr + (k_start + k_off[:, None]) * stride_rhs_k + (N_start + n_off[None, :]) * stride_rhs_n
+        rhs_tile = tl.load(rhs_ptrs)
+
+        acc += tl.dot(lhs_tile, rhs_tile)
+
+    # Store to global memory
+    m_off = tl.arange(0, TM)
+    n_off = tl.arange(0, TN)
+    return acc
+
+
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+# Single-chunk inter-chunk step kernel
+# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
+
+@triton.jit
+def _kda_chunk_step_kernel(
+    qe_n_ptr, w_n_ptr, u_n_ptr, aqk_n_ptr, kg_n_ptr, gl_n_ptr,
+    o_n_ptr, S_ptr,             # S is (K, V) in global memory
+    stride_S_k,
+    BT: tl.constexpr, K: tl.constexpr, V: tl.constexpr,
+    BATCH: tl.constexpr,         # = BH
+    TM: tl.constexpr, TN: tl.constexpr, TK: tl.constexpr,
+):
+    """
+    Single-chunk step for all (B,H) pairs.
+    Grid: (BATCH, cdiv(BT,TM), cdiv(V,TN))
+    Each program computes one TM×TN tile.
+
+    S is stored in global memory (L2-cached). Each batch has its own S.
+    """
+    batch_id = tl.program_id(0)
+    bt_tile = tl.program_id(1)
+    v_tile = tl.program_id(2)
+
+    # S pointer for this batch
+    S_b = S_ptr + batch_id * stride_S_k * V
+
+    bt_start = bt_tile * TM
+    bt_end = tl.minimum(bt_start + TM, BT)
+    v_start = v_tile * TN
+    v_end = tl.minimum(v_start + TN, V)
+
+    # Step 1: wS tile
+    ws_acc = tl.zeros([TM, TN], dtype=tl.float32)
+    for k_start in range(0, K, TK):
+        m_off = tl.arange(0, TM)
+        n_off = tl.arange(0, TN)
+        k_off = tl.arange(0, TK)
+
+        # w[tile] from global
+        w_ptrs = w_n_ptr + batch_id * (BT * K) + (bt_start + m_off[:, None]) * K + (k_start + k_off[None, :])
+        w_tile = tl.load(w_ptrs)
+
+        # S[tile] from global
+        s_ptrs = S_b + (k_start + k_off[:, None]) * V + (v_start + n_off[None, :])
+        s_tile = tl.load(s_ptrs)
+
+        ws_acc += tl.dot(w_tile, s_tile)
+
+    # Step 2: qS tile
+    qs_acc = tl.zeros([TM, TN], dtype=tl.float32)
+    for k_start in range(0, K, TK):
+        m_off = tl.arange(0, TM)
+        n_off = tl.arange(0, TN)
+        k_off = tl.arange(0, TK)
+
+        q_ptrs = qe_n_ptr + batch_id * (BT * K) + (bt_start + m_off[:, None]) * K + (k_start + k_off[None, :])
+        q_tile = tl.load(q_ptrs)
+
+        s_ptrs = S_b + (k_start + k_off[:, None]) * V + (v_start + n_off[None, :])
+        s_tile = tl.load(s_ptrs)
+
+        qs_acc += tl.dot(q_tile, s_tile)
+
+    # Step 3: vc = u - wS (element-wise)
+    m_off = tl.arange(0, TM)
+    n_off = tl.arange(0, TN)
+    u_ptrs = u_n_ptr + batch_id * (BT * V) + (bt_start + m_off[:, None]) * V + (v_start + n_off[None, :])
+    u_tile = tl.load(u_ptrs

... (2335 more chars)

+# Compiled version for each common NT value
+_inter_chunk_compiled = {}

+def _get_compiled_inter_chunk(NT_val):
+    if NT_val not in _inter_chunk_compiled:
+        # Generate a specialized function for this NT
+        exec_globals = {

+#include <cuda_bf16.h>
+#include <cuda_fp16.h>
+
+#define BT 64
+#define K_DIM 128
+#define V_DIM 128
+#define WARPS 4
+#define WARP_SIZE 32
+
+// FP32 FMA-based matmul kernel for inter-chunk recurrence.
+// Designed for small matrices (BT=64, K=128, V=128).
+// Each block handles one (B,H) pair. State S is in global memory (L2-cached).
+// Inputs (w, q, u, Aqk, kg, gl) are fp32.
+
+extern "C" __global__ void inter_chunk_kernel(
+    const float* __restrict__ qe,      // (BH, NT, BT, K)
+    const float* __restrict__ w,       // (BH, NT, BT, K)
+    const float* __restrict__ u,       // (BH, NT, BT, V)
+    const float* __restrict__ Aqk,     // (BH, NT, BT, BT)
+    const float* __restrict__ kg,      // (BH, NT, BT, K)
+    const float* __restrict__ gl,      // (BH, NT, K)
+    float* __restrict__ o,             // (BH, NT, BT, V)
+    float* __restrict__ S_global,      // (BH, K, V)
+    int BH,
+    int NT
+) {
+    int bh = blockIdx.x;
+    if (bh >= BH) return;
+
+    int tid = threadIdx.x;
+    int warp_id = tid / WARP_SIZE;
+    int lane_id = tid % WARP_SIZE;
+
+    int stride_main = NT * BT;
+    int stride_qe = stride_main * K_DIM;
+    int stride_w = stride_main * K_DIM;
+    int stride_u = stride_main * V_DIM;
+    int stride_aqk = stride_main * BT;
+    int stride_kg = stride_main * K_DIM;
+    int stride_gl = NT * K_DIM;
+    int stride_o = stride_main * V_DIM;
+    int stride_S = K_DIM * V_DIM;
+
+    const float* qe_bh  = qe  + bh * stride_qe;
+    const float* w_bh   = w   + bh * stride_w;
+    const float* u_bh   = u   + bh * stride_u;
+    const float* aqk_bh = Aqk + bh * stride_aqk;
+    const float* kg_bh  = kg  + bh * stride_kg;
+    const float* gl_bh  = gl  + bh * stride_gl;
+    float* o_bh         = o   + bh * stride_o;
+    float* S_bh         = S_global + bh * stride_S;
+
+    // Initialize S to zero
+    for (int i = tid; i < K_DIM * V_DIM; i += blockDim.x) {
+        S_bh[i] = 0.0f;
+    }
+    __syncthreads();
+
+    // Scratch buffers for vc (BT * V elements)
+    // We use global memory buffers pre-allocated by the caller.
+    // For this kernel, we store vc in a temporary region of o.
+    // Actually, we compute vc on-the-fly.
+
+    for (int n = 0; n < NT; n++) {
+        const float* qe_n  = qe_bh  + n * BT * K_DIM;
+        const float* w_n   = w_bh   + n * BT * K_DIM;
+        const float* u_n   = u_bh   + n * BT * V_DIM;
+        const float* aqk_n = aqk_bh + n * BT * BT;
+        const float* kg_n  = kg_bh  + n * BT * K_DIM;
+        const float* gl_n  = gl_bh  + n * K_DIM;
+        float* o_n         = o_bh   + n * BT * V_DIM;
+
+        // ---- Phase A: Compute wS and qS, store in o_n as temp ----
+        // We store wS in o_n temporarily, then overwrite with vc, then final output.
+
+        // Phase A1: wS stored in o_n
+        // Distribute BT rows across warps
+        int bt_per_warp = BT / WARPS;
+        int bt_start = warp_id * bt_per_warp;
+        int bt_end = bt_start + bt_per_warp;
+
+        for (int bi = bt_start; bi < bt_end; bi++) {
+            for (int vj = 0; vj < V_DIM; vj++) {
+                float ws_sum = 0.0f;
+                for (int kk = 0; kk < K_DIM; kk++) {
+                    ws_sum += w_n[bi * K_DIM + kk] * S_bh[kk * V_DIM + vj];
+                }
+                o_n[bi * V_DIM + vj] = ws_sum;
+            }
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // Phase A2: Compute qS (keep in registers), compute vc = u - wS, store vc in o_n
+        for (int bi = bt_start; bi < bt_end; bi++) {
+            // First compute qS for this row into registers
+            float qs_row[V_DIM];
+            float vc_row[V_DIM];
+
+            for (int vj = 0; vj < V_DIM; vj++) {
+                // Read wS from o_n
+                float ws_val = o_n[bi * V_DIM + vj];
+
+                // vc = u - wS
+                vc_row[vj] = u_n[bi * V_DIM + vj] - ws_val;
+
+                // qS
+                float qs_sum = 0.0f;
+                for (int kk = 0; kk < K_DIM; kk++) {
+                    qs_sum += qe_n[bi * K_DIM + kk] * S_bh[kk * V_DIM + vj];
+                }
+                qs_row[vj] = qs_sum;
+
+                // Store vc in o_n (overwrite wS)
+                o_n[bi * V_DIM + vj] = vc_row[vj];
+            }
+
+            // Phase A3: Compute Aqk @ vc for this row, accumulate with qS, store final output
+            // Need to read vc[t, vj] for all t <= bi. vc is now stored in o_n.
+            for (int vj = 0; vj < V_DIM; vj++) {
+                float aqk_sum = 0.0f;
+                for (int t = 0; t <= bi; t++) {
+                    aqk_sum += aqk_n[bi * BT + t] * o_n[t * V_DIM + vj];
+                }
+                o_n[bi * V_DIM + vj] = qs_row[vj] + aqk_sum;
+            }
+        }
+        __syncthreads();
+
+        // ---- Phase B: State update ----
+        // The output o_n now holds the final output AND vc has been overwritten.
+        // We need vc for the state update, but we just overwrote o_n!
+        // This is a bug — we need to preserve vc.
+
+        // Let's recompute vc in this phase.
+        // Actually, we need to restructure: compute vc first, then output, then state update.
+        // OR: store vc in a separate buffer.
+
+        // For the state update, we need vc[t, vj] for all t.
+        // But o_n has been overwritten with final output.
+        // We need to store vc separately.
+
+        // For now: recompute wS from S and recompute vc.
+        // This is wasteful but correct.
+        for (int bi = bt_start; bi < bt_end; bi++) {
+            for (int vj = 0; vj < V_DIM; vj++) {
+                // Recomute wS
+                float ws_sum = 0.0f;
+                for (int kk = 0; kk < K_DIM; kk++) {
+                    ws_sum += w_n[bi * K_DIM + kk] * S_bh[kk * V_DIM + vj];
+                }
+                // vc = u - wS (recomputed)
+                float vc_val =
